Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited, what are the criteria for being considered a Muslim whose blood and wealth are protected?
A) Performing Hajj and fasting.
B) Saying the shahada, praying our prayer, facing our qibla, eating our slaughtered meat.
C) Being free from sin.
D) Living in a Muslim country.

Key Takeaway
Outward practice of Islam (shahada, prayer, qibla, slaughter) are the criteria for Muslim identity, and major sins do not remove that identity as long as one acknowledges the truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you consider yourself a believer even when you commit a major sin, or do you feel your faith is lost? Look at the condition set by the Prophet (peace be upon him): as long as you acknowledge the truth and do not deny it, you remain a Muslim. Will you let your sins drive you away from the community, or will you hold onto the outward signs of Islam and seek forgiveness?
?
When you see a fellow Muslim committing a major sin, do you treat them as an outsider or do you remember the protection of Allah and His Messenger over them? The hadith says 'do not violate Allah's protection concerning him.' How does this lesson change the way you view and interact with those who sin openly?
?
The Prophet fought people until they said la ilaha illallah and prayed. But once they did, their blood and wealth became protected, and their account is with Allah. Are you quick to judge others' inner faith, or do you leave their account to Allah? How can you apply this lesson to stop condemning others and focus on your own relationship with Allah?

The Prohibition of Disputing in Religious Matters
Imam Maydani presents the Sunni stance on theological debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunnah forbids arguing about Allah and the Quran, including the story of Abu Hanifa's ruling on disputing.

Core Idea
Disputing in religion is a blameworthy innovation, even if your position is correct.

This lesson presents a foundational principle of Ahl al-Sunnah: the prohibition of delving into Allah's essence and arguing about the Quran. Through the words of Imam Abu Hanifa and a famous incident, we learn that the very act of disputing is considered an innovation (bid'ah), regardless of whether one's belief is correct. The passage also affirms the Quran as the uncreated speech of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses in the Arabic; they indicate commentary inserted into the original text.
2
Identify the three prohibitions
The passage begins with three prohibitions: not delving into Allah, not disputing in the religion, and not arguing about the Quran. Find each one and note how they are defined.
3
Analyze the story of Abu Hanifa
Read the anecdote from Abu Yusuf. Pay attention to why Abu Hanifa forbade praying behind both men, even the one with the correct belief (that the Quran is uncreated).
This story illustrates that the act of disputing itself is blameworthy.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this teaching applies to contemporary debates. The point is not to avoid seeking truth, but to avoid contentious argumentation that leads to division.
Ahl al-Sunnah prioritizes unity and avoids speculative theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا نَخُوضُ فِي اللهِ وَلَا تُمَارِي فِي دِينِ اللهِ تَعَالَى وَلَا تُجَادِلُ فِي القُرْآنِ، وَنَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُ كَلامُ رَبِّ العَالَمِينَ نَزَلَ بِهِ الرُّوحُ الأَمِينُ فَعَلَّمَهُ سَيِّدَ المُرْسَلِينَ مُحَمَّدًا صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَعَلَى آلِهِ وَصَحْبِهِ أَجْمَعِينَ، وَكَلَامُ الله تَعَالَى لَا يُسَاوِيهِ شَيْءٌ مِنْ كَلَامِ المَخْلُوقِينَ. (و) مما يجب علينا أننا لا نخوض في ( ذات (الله) تعالى؛ روي عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال: «لا ينبغي لأحد أن ينطق في ذات الله بشيء بل يصفه بما وصف به نفسه. (ولا نماري) أي لا نداهن وهي عدم المبالاة (في دين الله تعالى، ولا نجـادل) أحدًا في القرآن العظيم؛ بل نعتقد (ونعلم أنه كلام رب العالمين نزل به جبرائيل (الروح الأمين فعلمه سيد الأنبياء والمرسلين نبينا محمدا صلى الله ) عليه وعلى آله الأكرمين ( وصحبه أجمعين)؛ روي عن أبي يوسف أنه قال: كنت عند أبي حنيفة إذ دخل عليه جماعة في أيديهم رجلان فقالوا: إن أحد هذين يقول : إن القرآن مخلوق والآخر ينازعه ويقول: القرآن غير مخلوق؛ فقال : لا تصلوا خلفهما، فقلت: أما الذي يقول القرآن مخلوق فنعم؛ لأنه لا يقول بقدم القرآن، وأما الآخر فما باله لا يصلى خلفه؟ قال: إنهما تنازعا في الدين والمنازعة في الدين بدعة. و كلام الله تعالى لا يساويه) ولا يشبهه (شيء من كلام المخلوقين)؛ لأنه صفة من صفات رب العالمين، وقد تقدم لك أن من شَبَّه من صفات الله بشيء من صفات المخلوقين كان من الكافرين.
And we do not delve into Allah, nor do we dispute in the religion of Allah, the Exalted, nor do we argue about the Quran. And we know that it is the speech of the Lord of the worlds, the Trustworthy Spirit brought it down and taught it to the master of the messengers, Muhammad, may Allah bless him and his family and all his companions. And the speech of Allah, the Exalted, nothing from the speech of creation equals it. (And) from what is obligatory upon us is that we do not delve into the essence of Allah, the Exalted; it is narrated from Abu Hanifa that he said: 'It is not befitting for anyone to speak about the essence of Allah with anything, but rather he describes Him as He has described Himself.' (And we do not dispute) meaning we do not dissimulate, and it is the lack of concern (in the religion of Allah, the Exalted, and we do not argue) with anyone about the Mighty Quran; rather we believe (and we know that it is the speech of the Lord of the worlds, Jibril brought it down, the Trustworthy Spirit taught it to the master of the prophets and messengers, our Prophet Muhammad, may Allah bless him and on his noble family and all his companions); it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he said: I was with Abu Hanifa when a group entered upon them with two men, and they said: 'One of these two says that the Quran is created, and the other disputes with him and says the Quran is uncreated.' He said: 'Do not pray behind either of them.' I said: 'As for the one who says the Quran is created, yes, because he does not assert the pre-eternity of the Quran. But as for the other, why should one not pray behind him?' He said: 'They both disputed in the religion, and disputing in the religion is an innovation.' And the speech of Allah, the Exalted, nothing from the speech of creation equals it nor resembles it; because it is an attribute from the attributes of the Lord of the worlds, and it has preceded for you that whoever likens any of Allah's attributes to something from the attributes of creation is among the disbelievers.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the Sunni stance regarding arguments about Allah and the Quran. It begins by stating three prohibitions: not delving into (khawd) Allah, not disputing (mirā') in the religion, and not arguing (jidal) about the Quran. Then it affirms the Quran as the uncreated speech of Allah, brought by Jibril and taught to the Prophet. The commentary explains that 'not delving into Allah' means speaking about His essence; a narration from Abu Hanifa forbids speaking about Allah's essence except as He described Himself. 'Not disputing' means not being apathetic or obstinate in religion. The passage then relates a story from Abu Yusuf: two men disputed about whether the Quran is created or uncreated, and Abu Hanifa forbade praying behind both because the act of disputing itself is an innovation (bid'ah). This shows that even the one with the correct belief (that the Quran is uncreated) is reprimanded for engaging in argument. Finally, it reiterates that Allah's speech is incomparable to created speech, and likening any attribute of Allah to creation is disbelief. The author follows the order: prohibitions, affirmation, proof from narration, and conclusion.

Key Terms
الخوض في الله
المماراة
المجادلة في القرآن
بدعة
صفات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is acceptable to argue in defense of correct Islamic beliefs.
Correction: Abu Hanifa forbade praying behind both disputants because the act of disputing itself is an innovation, regardless of who is correct.

Checkpoint
According to the story of Abu Hanifa, why did he forbid praying behind both men who disputed about the Quran?
A) Because the one who said the Quran is created was a disbeliever.
B) Because they both disputed in the religion, and disputing in the religion is an innovation.
C) Because the one who said the Quran is uncreated was a disbeliever.
D) Because they both held incorrect beliefs about the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah avoids theological disputation, considering it a bid'ah, and affirms the Quran as uncreated speech of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about religion, do you ever cross the line from sincere inquiry into disputation? Have you felt that inner thrill of being 'right' that Abu Hanifa warned against? Pause and ask yourself: do you defend the truth for Allah or for your own ego? Commit to checking your intention before you speak.
?
The passage says not to delve into Allah's essence. Is there a part of you that feels unsatisfied with what Allah has revealed about Himself? Do you find yourself wanting to 'understand' more than He permitted? This is a dangerous path. Are you willing to submit your intellect to His words, even when it feels incomplete?
?
Abu Hanifa forbade praying behind a man who held the correct belief because he disputed. Would you be willing to stop arguing even when you know you are right? Can you let go of the need to 'win' religious arguments for the sake of unity and avoiding innovation? Where in your life today can you practice silence and entrust the truth to Allah?

The Sunni Middle Path on Sin, Hope, and Despair
The author states the Sunni position on sin, hope, and despair.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni middle position on sin, hope, and despair, distinguishing it from the extremes of the Murji'ah, Mu'tazilah, and Khawarij.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah neither guarantee Paradise nor condemn sinners, but entrust their fate to Allah.

In this passage, Imam al-Tahawi clarifies the Sunni stance on the fate of sinful believers. He rejects both the overly optimistic view of the Murji'ah and the harsh judgment of the Khawarij and Mu'tazilah, instead teaching a balanced approach of hope and fear. This lesson unpacks that middle path and its implications for our understanding of faith and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Pay attention to the opening statement and the contrasts drawn between the different groups.
2
Identify the three theological positions
The passage mentions four groups: Murji'ah, Mu'tazilah, Khawarij, and Ahl al-Sunnah. Notice how each group views sin and its consequences for a believer.
The Murji'ah say sin does not harm faith; the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij say sinners will be punished; Ahl al-Sunnah take the middle path.
3
Understand the Sunni middle path
Ahl al-Sunnah neither decisively judge punishment nor reward for a believer. They hope for the good, fear for the sinner, and entrust the final outcome to Allah. This balanced view avoids both despair and false security.
The key terms are 'hope' (رجاء) and 'fear' (خوف) — not absolute assurance.
4
Reflect on testifying for Paradise
The passage explains that Ahl al-Sunnah do not testify for any individual that they are in Paradise. It then notes that the Salaf had three views, the first being that testimony is only for prophets. This shows caution in claiming salvation for anyone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في اقتراف الذنوب والأمن والإياس
وَلَا نَقُولُ: لَا يَضُرُّ مَعَ الإِسْلَامِ ذَنْبٌ لِمَنْ عَمِلَهُ، وَنَرْجُو لِلْمُحْسِنِينَ مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَلَا نَأْمَنُ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا نَشْهَدُ لَهُمْ بِالجَنَّةِ وَنَسْتَغْفِرُ لِمُسِيئِهِمْ وَنَخَافُ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا نُقَنِّطُهُمْ.
وَالأَمْنُ وَالإِيَاسُ يَنْقِلانِ عَنِ اللَّهِ، وَسَبِيلُ الحَقِّ بَيْنَهُمَا لِأَهْلِ القِبْلَةِ، وَلَا يَخْرُجُ العَبْدُ مِنَ الْإِيمَانِ إِلَّا بِجُحُودِ مَا أَدْخَلَهُ فِيهِ.
(ولا تقول: لا يضر مع الإسلام ذنب لمن عمله) خلافًا للمرجئة القائلين بأنه لا يضر مع الإيمان ذنب كما لا تنفع مع الكفر حسنة، فحسناتنا مقبولة وسيئاتنا مغفورة، وبائنتهم [؟] المعتزلة والخوارج فقطعوا بعقابه، وتوسطت أهل السنة فلم يقطعوا بعقاب ولا ثواب لعاصي ولا لأواب، بل فوضوا أمره إلى رب الأرباب (و) قالوا: (نرجو) أي نؤمل من فضل الله إنجاز ما وعده للمحسنين من المؤمنين، و لكن (لا) نأمن عليهم مكر الله تعالى؛ إذ لا يأمن مكر الله إلا القوم الخاسرون (ولا نشهد لهم بالجنة) بما هم لها مقدمون وبها إن قبلت أعمالهم موعودون.
واعلم أن للسلف في الشهادة بالجنة ثلاثة أقوال: أحدها: ألا يُشهد لأحد إلا للأنبياء، وهذا ينقل عن محمد ابن الحنفية (1)
(1) محمد بن الإمام علي بن أبي طالب أبو القاسم أخو الحسن والحسين، وأمه من سبي اليمامة [؟] من أبي بكر، وهي خولة بنت جعفر الحنفية، وقد جمع له بين الاسم والكنية ترخيصاً من النبي على ما روي، ولد في العام الذي مات فيه أبو بكر ورأى الصحابة وروى عنهم، وكان نهاية في العلم غاية في العبادة، وكانت الشيعة في زمانه تتغالى فيه وتدعى إمامته، وهو عند الكيسانية المهدي ويزعمون أنه لم يمت، توفي سنة ٨١هـ. انظر ترجمته في سير أعلام النبلاء (٤/١١٠)، شذرات الذهب (٣٣٠/١-٣٣٢).
Discussion on committing sins and security and despair. And we do not say: sin does not harm with Islam for whoever commits it. And we hope for the good-doers from the believers, and we do not feel secure for them, and we do not testify for them with Paradise, and we ask forgiveness for their wrongdoers, and we fear for them, and we do not cause them to despair. And security and despair take one out of the religion of Allah, and the path of truth between them is for the people of the Qibla. And the servant does not exit from faith except by denial of that which admitted him into it. (And do not say: sin does not harm with Islam for whoever commits it) contrary to the Murji'ah who say that sin does not harm with faith just as no good deed benefits with disbelief, so our good deeds are accepted and our evil deeds are forgiven. And in contrast [?] the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij, so they decisively judged his punishment. And Ahl al-Sunnah took the middle path, so they did not decisively judge punishment nor reward for a disobedient person nor for a repentant one, but rather they entrusted his affair to the Lord of lords. And they said: (We hope) i.e., we hope from Allah's grace the fulfillment of what He promised to the good-doers from the believers, but (we do not) feel secure for them against the plot of Allah the Exalted; for none feels secure against the plot of Allah except the losing people. (And we do not testify for them with Paradise) because they are advancing towards it and, if their deeds are accepted, they are promised with it. And know that for the Salaf there are three sayings regarding testifying for Paradise: One of them: that one should not testify for anyone except the prophets, and this is narrated from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah. (1) (Footnote): Muhammad ibn al-Imam Ali ibn Abi Talib Abu al-Qasim, brother of al-Hasan and al-Husayn, and his mother was from the captives of al-Yamama [?] from Abu Bakr, and she was Khawlah bint Ja'far al-Hanafiyyah, and he was given both the name and the kunya as a dispensation from the Prophet according to what was narrated. He was born in the year in which Abu Bakr died, and he saw the Companions and narrated from them, and he was extreme in knowledge and utmost in worship, and the Shia in his time used to exaggerate about him and claim his imamate, and he is with the Kaysaniyya the Mahdi and they claim that he did not die. He died in the year 81 AH. See his biography in Siyar A'lam al-Nubala' (4/110), Shadharat al-Dhahab (1/330-332).
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the creedal text from the Tahawiyyah concerning the position on sins, hope, and despair. It begins by quoting the matn: 'We do not say: sin does not harm with Islam for whoever commits it.' This directly opposes the Murji'ah, who held that sin does not harm a believer just as no good deed benefits a disbeliever. The commentary then explains that the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij took the extreme opposite stance, decisively judging punishment for sinners. Ahl al-Sunnah, however, take the middle path: they neither guarantee Paradise for believers nor condemn them to hell definitively, but rather entrust their fate to Allah. The author then unpacks the phrase 'we hope'—meaning they hope for Allah's promised reward for the righteous—and 'we do not feel secure'—because only the losers feel secure from Allah's plan. Similarly, they do not testify for any individual that they are in Paradise, as that is conditional upon acceptance of deeds. Finally, the author notes that the Salaf had three views on testifying for Paradise; only the first is mentioned here, attributed to Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah, which restricts such testimony to prophets alone. The footnote provides a brief biography of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah.

المعتزلة
الخوارج

الإياس
الشهادة بالجنة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ahl al-Sunnah guarantee Paradise for all believers.
Correction: The passage shows they do not testify for any individual except by explicit textual evidence, and they entrust the final outcome to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah regarding the punishment of a sinful believer?
A) They decisively judge that the sinner will be punished.
B) They decisively judge that the sinner will be forgiven.
C) They neither decisively judge punishment nor reward, but entrust the matter to Allah.
D) They remain silent and do not give any opinion.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah neither decisively judge punishment nor reward for believers, but entrust their fate to Allah, maintaining a balance of hope and fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you lean towards the Murji'ah extreme of 'it doesn't matter, I'm a believer anyway' or towards despair? How does the balanced path of Ahl al-Sunnah expose your tendency and call you to both hope and fear?
?
Do you find yourself feeling secure from Allah's punishment because of your good deeds or Islamic identity? How does the statement 'لا نأمن عليهم مكر الله' (we do not feel secure from Allah's plot) challenge that comfort zone?
?
Think of a Muslim you know who is struggling with a major sin. Do you tend to judge them (like Mu'tazilah) or excuse them completely (like Murji'ah)? How should the Sunni position of 'we fear for them, but we do not cause them to despair' change your attitude and actions towards them?

The Middle Path on Sin and Faith
Refuting Murji'ah and presenting the Ahl al-Sunnah middle position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah middle position between the extremes of Murji'ah and Mu'tazilah/Khawarij regarding sin and faith.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah avoid both reckless hope and despair, entrusting each person's fate to Allah.

This lesson addresses a key sectarian debate: does sin harm a believer's faith? The Murji'ah say no, while the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij are certain of punishment. Ahl al-Sunnah take a middle path, neither presuming safety nor despairing, but leaving the matter to Allah's wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage aloud, then the English translation. Notice the clear contrasts between the three positions.
2
Identify the three sects mentioned
The passage names the Murji'ah, Mu'tazilah, and Khawarij. Understand what each claims about sin and faith.
Murji'ah say sin does not harm with faith; Mu'tazilah and Khawarij are certain of punishment.
3
Locate the Ahl al-Sunnah middle course
The phrase 'و توسطت أهل السنة' marks the middle path. Notice they entrust the matter to Allah without definitive judgment.
4
Reflect on the practical implication
Consider how this balanced view affects a Muslim's attitude toward sin and repentance.
It encourages hope in Allah's mercy without complacency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تقول: لا يضر مع الإسلام ذنب لمن عمله) خلافًا للمرجئة القائلين بأنه لا يضر مع الإيمان ذنب كما لا تنفع مع الكفر حسنة، فحسناتنا مقبولة وسيئاتنا مغفورة، وبدعة المعتزلة والخوارج فقطعوا بعقابه، وتوسطت أهل السنة فلم يقطعوا بعقاب ولا ثواب لعاصي ولا لأواب، بل فوضوا أمره إلى رب الأرباب (و) قالوا: (نرجو) أي نؤمل من فضل الله إنجاز ما وعده للمحسنين من المؤمنين، و لكن (لا) نأمن عليهم مكر الله تعالى؛ إذ لا يأمن مكر الله إلا القوم الخاسرون (ولا نشهد لهم بالجنة) بما هم لها مقدمون وبها إن قبلت أعمالهم موعودون
(And we do not say: No sin harms with Islam for the one who commits it) contrary to the Murji'ah who say that no sin harms with faith just as no good deed benefits with disbelief. So our good deeds are accepted and our evil deeds are forgiven. And the innovation of the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij is that they definitely judged punishment upon the sinner. And Ahl al-Sunnah adopted a middle course: they do not definitely judge punishment nor reward for a sinner nor for an obedient person, rather they entrust his matter to the Lord of lords. And they said: 'We hope' i.e. we expect from Allah's bounty the fulfillment of what He has promised for the doers of good among the believers, but we do not feel secure from Allah's subtle plan (makra) for them, for none feels secure from Allah's subtle plan except the losing people. And we do not testify for them for Paradise, since they are brought to it and promised it if their deeds are accepted.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines the Ahl al-Sunnah position on sin and faith, contrasting it with three deviant sects. The author begins by quoting the matn's negation that sin harms with Islam. He explains that this contradicts the Murji'ah who claim sin never harms with faith just as good deeds never benefit with disbelief. Then he notes the innovation of the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij who definitely judge that the sinner will be punished. In between, Ahl al-Sunnah take a middle path: they do not definitively judge punishment or reward for any individual, but entrust their affair to Allah. Next, the author explains the Ahl al-Sunnah approach to hope and fear: they hope for Allah's bounty for the righteous, but do not feel secure from His subtle plan (citing Quran). They also do not testify for Paradise for any particular believer, as that is promised only conditionally upon acceptance of deeds.

أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ahl al-Sunnah are either too lenient (like Murji'ah) or too harsh (like Khawarij).
Correction: Ahl al-Sunnah take a middle path, not judging final outcomes but relying on Allah's justice and mercy.

Checkpoint

A) They judge punishment for all sinners
B) They do not definitively judge punishment or reward for any individual
C) They deny that sin has any effect on faith
D) They guarantee Paradise for all believers

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah avoid both extremes by neither dismissing the harm of sin nor presuming to know Allah's final judgment on any individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This week, which tendency will you consciously fight against, and what specific action will you take to replace it with the middle path of hope and fear?
?

Hope and Fear: The Balanced Approach
Explaining the Ahl al-Sunnah stance on hope, security, and testimony of Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunnah balance of hoping for Allah's mercy while fearing His plan, and why they do not testify to Paradise for any individual.

Core Idea
Hope without security, fear without despair — the believer's path between presumption and hopelessness.

After establishing the middle position on sin and faith, the author explains the proper attitude toward believers: we hope for their good, but never feel secure from Allah's subtle plan. Moreover, we do not testify that any particular believer will enter Paradise, as that promise is conditional on acceptance of deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the verbs 'نرجو' (we hope) and 'لا نأمن' (we do not feel secure).
2
Understand the meaning of 'hope' here
Hope here is a confident expectation of Allah's mercy, not mere wishful thinking. It is based on His promise to the righteous.
3
Examine the Quranic quote
The phrase 'لا يأمن مكر الله إلا القوم الخاسرون' is from Surah Al-A'raf (7:99). Reflect on why feeling secure from Allah's plan is condemned.
4
Grasp the conditional nature of Paradise
The passage states that Paradise is promised, but only if deeds are accepted. This is why Ahl al-Sunnah do not testify for any individual.

Source Text

Three Opinions on Testifying for Paradise
Enumeration of three opinions on testifying for Paradise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions held by the Salaf on testifying for Paradise and the evidence each opinion relies on.

Core Idea
Testifying for Paradise is not uniform; scholars differed based on evidence.

This passage from the Sharh al-Tahawiyyah presents three views of the early Muslims on who can be testified for Paradise. It sets a precedent for theological caution and reliance on textual evidence. As you read, notice how each opinion is anchored in a different source: definitive ruling, scriptural promise, or community witness.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the basic meaning.
Pay attention to the structure: it starts with 'اعلم أن للسلف' and then lists three opinions.
2
Identify the three opinions
List each opinion and its supporting evidence as mentioned in the passage. Note which scholars or hadith are cited for each.
The first opinion is definitive (قطعي) according to its supporters.
3
Reflect on the balance
Consider how the third opinion uses community testimony as evidence. Think about why scholars differed and what principles guide each view.
This connects to the broader topic of faith and testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن للسلف في الشهادة بالجنة ثلاثة أقوال: أحدها: ألا يُشهد لأحد إلا للأنبياء، وهذا ينقل عن محمد ابن الحنفية والأوزاعي، وهذا أمر قطعي لا نزاع فيه. والثاني: أن يُشهد لكل مؤمن جاء نص في حقه، وهو قول كثير من العلماء وعليه المصنف كما سيأتي. والثالث: أن يُشهد لمن شهد له المؤمنون كما في الصحيحين أنه مر بجنازة فأثنوا عليها بخير فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «وَجَبَتْ» ومـر بأخرى فأثني عليها بشر فقال: «وَجَبَتْ»؛ فقال عمر : يا رسول الله ما وجبت؟ فقال: «هَذَا أَثْنَيْتُم عَلَيْهِ خَيْرًا فَوَجَبَتْ لَهُ الجنَّةُ وَهَذَا أَثْنَيْتُم عَلَيْهِ شَرًّا فَوَجَبَتْ لَهُ النَّارُ أَنْتُمْ شُهَدَاءُ الله تَعَالَى فِي الْأَرْضِ»
And know that the Salaf have three opinions regarding testifying for Paradise: One of them: that one should not testify for anyone except the prophets. This is transmitted from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah and al-Awza'i, and this matter is definitive with no dispute in it. The second: that one should testify for every believer concerning whom a text has come. This is the saying of many scholars, and the author (al-Tahawi) adopts it as will come. The third: that one should testify for those for whom the believers testify, as in the two Sahihs: that a funeral passed by and they praised it with good, so he (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'It is obligated.' And another passed by and they blamed it with evil, so he said: 'It is obligated.' So 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'O Messenger of Allah, what is obligated?' He said: 'This one you praised with good, so Paradise is obligated for him; and this one you blamed with evil, so the Fire is obligated for him. You are Allah's witnesses on earth.'
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the three opinions of the Salaf (early righteous predecessors) on the issue of testifying for Paradise for specific individuals. The author, al-Maydani, begins by stating that the Salaf held three views. The first view: only the prophets can have Paradise testified for them; this is definitive according to those who hold it, and it is transmitted from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah and al-Awza'i. The second view: one can testify for any believer about whom a specific text (from Quran or hadith) has come promising Paradise, such as the ten promised Paradise; this is the view of many scholars and the position of al-Tahawi himself, as will be explained later. The third view: one can testify for a person if the Muslim community bears witness to his goodness, based on the hadith from the two Sahihs where the Prophet said 'وجبت' when people praised a deceased person. The hadith is quoted fully to support this view.

Key Terms
الشهادة بالجنة
السلف
محمد ابن الحنفية
الأوزاعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That testifying for Paradise is a uniform practice; in fact, scholars differed based on evidence.
Correction: The Salaf held three distinct opinions: only for prophets, for those with specific textual promises, and for those the community testifies for.

Checkpoint
What is the first opinion mentioned regarding testifying for Paradise?
A) Only for the prophets
B) For every believer with a textual promise
C) For anyone the community praises
D) For all Muslims

Key Takeaway
The Salaf held three distinct views on testifying for Paradise, each grounded in evidence: restricted to prophets, based on textual promises, or based on community testimony.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to die today, would your fellow believers honestly and confidently testify for your goodness? What steps are you taking to deserve such testimony?
?
The Prophet ﷺ said you are Allah's witnesses on earth. Are you careful about the testimonies you give about others, especially the deceased? Do you speak good of them only when you can truthfully do so?
?
The first opinion restricts testimony for Paradise to prophets. Does this make you rely more on Allah's mercy than on your own deeds or others' praise? How can you cultivate that reliance today?

The Balanced Path: Between Security and Despair
The author states the Ahl al-Sunnah position on security and despair, citing Quranic support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both extreme security from Allah's plan and absolute despair of His mercy remove a person from the Islamic religion, and how Ahl al-Sunnah maintains a balanced path.

Core Idea
Extreme hope and extreme despair both lead out of Islam; the middle path is the way of Ahl al-Sunnah.

This passage from the Sharh al-Tahawiyyah addresses two dangerous extremes: feeling completely secure from Allah's subtle plan, and despairing of His mercy. Both are condemned in the Quran and remove one from the religion. The author emphasizes that the correct path lies between them, and this is the position of the people of the qiblah. Let's examine the scriptural basis and the balanced approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Notice how Ahl al-Sunnah approaches sinners: seeking forgiveness, fearing for them, but not cutting off hope or making them despair.
This sets the tone of balance.
2
Identify the two extremes
Locate the terms 'al-amn' (security) and 'al-i'yas' (despair). Understand what each means in this context.
3
Note the Quranic evidence
The passage cites that only disbelievers despair of Allah's spirit. Reflect on how this supports the ruling.
Quran 12:87 is referenced.
4
Understand the balanced path
The author states that the path of truth is between the two extremes. Consider why this balance is essential in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونستغفر للمسيئين ونخاف عليهم مما أعد لهم (ولا نقطعهم) ونؤيسهم من رحمة الله تعالى؛ إذ لا ييأس من روح الله إلا القوم الكافرون (والأمن) من مكر الله تعالى (والإياس) من روح الله ينقلان عن الملة الإسلامية لما تلونا، وسبيل الحق بينهما، وهو القول لأهل القبلة.
وفي شرح العقائد: «فإن قيل: الجزم بأن العاصي يكون في النار يأس من الله تعالى وبأن المطيع يكون في الجنة أمن من الله تعالى فيكون المعتزلي كافرا مطيعا كان أو عاصيا؛ لأنه إما أمن أو آيس، ومن قواعد أهل السنة: ألا يُكفّر أحد من أهل القبلة. قلنا: هذا ليس بيأس ولا أمن؛ لأنه على تقدير العصيان لا ييأس أن يوفقه الله تعالى للتوبة والعمل الصالح، وعلى تقدير الطاعة لا يأمن أن يخذله فيكتسب المعاصي»
And we seek forgiveness for the sinners and we fear for them what has been prepared for them, and we do not cut them off (from hope), and we do not make them despair of Allah's mercy, for none despairs of Allah's spirit except the disbelieving people. And security from Allah's subtle plan and despair of Allah's spirit remove one from the Islamic religion, as we have recited (the verses). And the path of truth is between them, and that is the saying of the people of the qiblah.
And in Sharh al-Aqa'id: 'If it is said: The definitive judgment that the sinner will be in the Fire is despair of Allah, and that the obedient will be in Paradise is security from Allah, thus the Mu'tazili becomes a disbeliever whether he is obedient or disobedient because he either feels security or despair. And from the principles of Ahl al-Sunnah is that no one from the people of the qiblah is declared a disbeliever. We say: This is neither despair nor security, because on the assumption of disobedience, he does not despair that Allah will grant him success to repentance and righteous deeds; and on the assumption of obedience, he does not feel secure that Allah will abandon him so that he commits sins.'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, addressing the concepts of security (al-amn) and despair (al-i'yas). The author first explains that Ahl al-Sunnah seek forgiveness for sinners and fear for them the punishment prepared, but they do not sever hope, nor do they make them despair of Allah's mercy, citing the Quranic verse that only disbelievers despair of Allah's spirit. He then states that both extreme security from Allah's subtle plan and extreme despair of His mercy remove a person from the Islamic religion, as earlier recited verses indicate. The correct path is between them, which is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah. The author then quotes from 'Sharh al-Aqa'id' an objection-and-answer: The objection argues that the Mu'tazili's definite judgment that a sinner goes to Hell is despair, and that the obedient goes to Paradise is security, thus the Mu'tazili becomes a disbeliever under either condition, conflicting with the Ahl al-Sunnah principle of not excommunicating anyone from the qiblah. The answer clarifies that this is not true despair or security, because on the possibility of disobedience, they do not despair of Allah granting tawfiq for repentance; on the possibility of obedience, they do not feel secure that Allah might abandon them to sins. Hence their definitive judgment is not based on the actual outcome but on the prevailing apparent condition, so it does not entail kufr.

Key Terms
الأمن
الإياس
ملة الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ahl al-Sunnah's refusal to declare Muslims in hell or heaven leads to moral indifference.
Correction: On the contrary, Ahl al-Sunnah maintain a balance: they fear Allah's punishment for sinners but do not despair of His mercy, and they hope for the obedient but do not feel secure from His plan.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a person who feels completely secure from Allah's subtle plan?
A) He is still a believer
B) He is removed from the Islamic religion
C) He is sinful but remains Muslim
D) He is a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
Both extreme security from Allah's plan and utter despair of His mercy are grounds for leaving Islam; the correct path is a balanced hope and fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself feeling so secure in your good deeds that you neglected repentance, or so despairing of your sins that you gave up hope? Which extreme do you lean towards, and what one concrete step will you take today to move toward the balanced path of Ahl al-Sunnah?
?
If you truly believed that both security from Allah's plan and despair of His mercy could remove you from Islam, how would it change the way you evaluate your own heart state each day?
?
Think of a sin you struggle with. Does your inner response tend toward feeling 'it's too late for me' or 'Allah will forgive me anyway'? How can the lesson's warning about both extremes reshape your repentance tonight?

The Objection: Does Definitive Judgment Imply Kufr?
The author presents an objection and its resolution regarding Mu'tazili definitive judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that Mu'tazili definitive judgments about Heaven and Hell constitute security or despair, and the Ahl al-Sunnah response that such judgments are not actual despair or security.

Core Idea
Definitive judgment on a person's final outcome does not constitute security or despair because it is based on apparent state, not actual outcome.

In the commentary, the author quotes a discussion from Sharh al-Aqa'id addressing a potential inconsistency: if Ahl al-Sunnah do not excommunicate anyone from the qiblah, but the Mu'tazili definitively judges that a sinner is in Hell (despair) and an obedient is in Paradise (security), does that not make the Mu'tazili a disbeliever? The answer clarifies the nature of these judgments.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: The Mu'tazili's definitive judgment that a sinner is in Hell and an obedient in Paradise is claimed to be despair and security, respectively.
2
Understand the Ahl al-Sunnah principle
Note the principle that no one from the people of the qiblah is declared a disbeliever. This is a key tenet.
3
Examine the response
Read the answer: The judgment is not true despair or security because possibilities of change remain (repentance or falling into sin).
4
Reflect on the balance
Consider how this response maintains the balance between hope and fear without falling into extremism.

Source Text

Second and Third Types of Testimony for Believers
Lists the second and third types of shahada with hadith evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third types of testimony that Ahl al-Sunna give for believers and the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Testimony for a believer can be based on explicit texts or the community's witness.

After establishing the general testimony for all believers, the author now specifies two additional categories: those for whom a scriptural text exists and those whom the believing community praises. The Prophet's hadith about the two funerals demonstrates that the collective witness of Muslims is a valid basis for testifying to a person's ultimate destiny.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second type
Learn that the second type of testimony is for every believer for whom an explicit scriptural text (nass) exists, such as the ten promised Paradise.
This type is based on direct revelation, not inference.
2
Understand the third type
Learn that the third type is based on the collective praise (thana') of the Muslim community for a deceased person.
The evidence is the hadith of the two funerals.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Read the hadith from Bukhari and Muslim where the Prophet declares Paradise obligated for the one praised and Hell for the one criticized, and explains that the community's witness is Allah's testimony on earth.
This hadith establishes communal shahada as valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن يُشهد لكل مؤمن جاء نص في حقه، وهو قول كثير من العلماء وعليه المصنف كما سيأتي. والثالث: أن يُشهد لمن شهد له المؤمنون كما في الصحيحين أنه مر بجنازة فأثنوا عليها بخير فقال عليه الصلاة والسلام: «وَجَبَتْ» ومر بأخرى فأثني عليها بشر فقال: «وَجَبَتْ»؛ فقال عمر: يا رسول الله ما وجبت؟ فقال: «هَذَا أَثْنَيْتُم عَلَيْهِ خَيْرًا فَوَجَبَتْ لَهُ الجَنَّةَ وَهَذَا أَثْنَيْتُم عَلَيْهِ شَرًّا فَوَجَبَتْ لَهُ النَّارَ أَنْتُمْ شُهَدَاءُ اللهِ تَعَالَى فِي الْأَرْضِ»
And the second: that testimony be given for every believer for whom a text has come regarding his right, and this is the saying of many scholars and upon it is the author as will come. And the third: that testimony be given for whom the believers testify, as in the two Sahihs that he (ﷺ) passed by a funeral and they praised its occupant well, so he said: 'It is obligated.' And he passed by another and they praised its occupant badly, so he said: 'It is obligated.' So ‘Umar said: O Messenger of Allah, what is obligated? He said: 'This one, you praised him well, so Paradise is obligated for him, and this one, you praised him badly, so the Fire is obligated for you are Allah's witnesses on earth.'
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage explains the second and third types of testimony (shahada) that Ahl al-Sunna give for believers. The first type, mentioned earlier, is the absolute testimony for all believers. The second type is testimony for a specific believer when there is an explicit scriptural text (nass) indicating his good end, such as the ten promised Paradise. The author notes that many scholars hold this view, and the author of the Tahawiyyah will later affirm it. The third type is testimony based on the consensus of the Muslim community’s praise for a deceased person. The evidence is the well-known hadith in Bukhari and Muslim: the Prophet passed by two funerals; upon the first, companions praised the deceased, and he said 'wajabat' (it is obligated), meaning Paradise is obligated. Upon the second, they criticized him, and he said 'wajabat,' meaning the Fire is obligated. When ‘Umar asked for explanation, the Prophet stated that their praise or blame constitutes testimony on earth, and Allah accepts it. This shows that communal witness can be a basis for testimony about a person's ultimate destiny.

Key Terms
شهادة
نص
ثناء
وجبت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that testimony for believers is only based on explicit texts.
Correction: The lesson shows that communal witness also serves as valid testimony, as established by the hadith.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the basis for the third type of testimony for a believer?
A) An explicit text from the Qur'an or hadith about that individual
B) The unanimous testimony of all Muslims throughout history
C) The collective praise of the Muslim community for the deceased
D) A dream or revelation received by a righteous person

Key Takeaway
For Ahl al-Sunna, testimony for a believer is not only through explicit texts but also through the collective witness of the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of a deceased Muslim you knew, on what basis do you testify for them? Is it because of their apparent deeds or because you know of an explicit text? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the grounds of your certainty about others?

The Balanced Path: Istighfar, Fear, and Hope for Sinners
Tahawi defines the Sunni position on istighfar, fear, and hope for sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna's balanced position on seeking forgiveness, fear, and hope for sinners, and why both despair and false security are outside the Islamic nation.

Core Idea
Hope and fear must be balanced; despair and false security both lead out of Islam.

The passage you are about to read comes from Imam al-Tahawi's creed. It addresses a crucial question: how should we view fellow Muslims who commit sins? Some fall into despair, thinking they are hopeless, while others grow overconfident and feel secure from Allah's plan. Tahawi lays out the Sunni middle path: we seek forgiveness for them, we fear for them, but we never cut them off from hope. This balanced approach is what defines Ahl al-Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify three actions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins with what we do (istighfar, fear) and then what we don't do (cut off, despair). Then he mentions two extremes that remove one from Islam. Finally, he states the middle path.
Pay attention to the verbs: 'nastaghfir', 'nakhaf', 'la naqta'uhum', 'la nu'isuhum'.
2
Understand the two extremes
The passage states that 'security from Allah's plan' (amn min makr Allah) and 'despair of Allah's spirit' (iyaas min rawh Allah) both remove one from the Islamic nation. Why? Because they contradict proper reliance on Allah. Reflect on the verses that might support this (e.g., Surah Yusuf 87, Surah Al-A'raf 99).
3
Grasp the middle path
The author concludes that the true saying is what is between these two extremes. This is the position of the 'people of the qibla' – all Muslims who pray toward Makkah. This balanced view avoids both complacency and hopelessness.
This is the defining characteristic of Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونستغفر المسيئهم ونخاف عليهم مما أعد لهم ولكن لا نقطهم ولا نؤيسهم من رحمة الله تعالى؛ إذ لا ييأس من روح الله إلا القوم الكافرون. والأمن من مكر الله تعالى والإياس من روح الله ينقلان عن الملة الإسلامية لما تلونا. وسبيل القول الحق ما بينهما، وهو قول أهل القبلة.
And we seek forgiveness for their wrongdoers and we fear for them from what has been prepared for them, but we do not cut them off nor make them despair of the mercy of Allah Almighty; for none despairs of the spirit of Allah except the disbelieving people. And security from the plan of Allah Almighty and despair of the spirit of Allah remove one from the Islamic nation as we have recited. And the path of the true saying is what is between them, and it is the saying of the people of the qibla.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the balanced position of Ahl al-Sunna regarding sinners. It begins by stating that we seek forgiveness (istighfar) for those among the believers who commit sins, and we fear for them the punishment prepared for them. However, we do not 'qat'ahum' (cut them off) or make them despair (yu'isahum) of Allah's mercy, because only the disbelievers despair of Allah's spirit (rawh). The phrase 'qat'ahum' likely means cutting them off from hope or from the community. Then it states that two extremes remove one from the Islamic nation: feeling secure from Allah's plan (amn min makr Allah) and despairing of Allah's mercy (iyaas min rawh Allah). The author refers to the verses that were recited earlier (presumably from Surah Yusuf and Surah Al-A'raf). The true path is the middle way between these two extremes, and that is the position of Ahl al-Sunna, the people of the qibla.

Key Terms
استغفار
خوف
رجاء
يأس
أمن
مكر الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fearing for a sinner means we must conclude they are going to Hell.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna fear for them but also hope for Allah's mercy and never despair; they balance fear and hope.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say about the one who feels secure from Allah's plan (amn min makr Allah)?
A) He is still a believer but sinful
B) He is removed from the Islamic nation
C) He is guilty of a minor sin
D) He is a hypocrite

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna maintain a balanced path: we seek forgiveness for sinners, fear for them, but never make them despair of Allah's mercy nor feel falsely secure from His plan.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does the warning 'security from the plan of Allah removes one from the Islamic nation' expose that arrogance in your heart, and what is one concrete step you will take today to replace it with humble fear?

The Rule for Leaving Faith: Juhud of What Brought One In
Resolves the apparent contradiction in takfir and states the general rule for leaving faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that only denial (juhud) of the foundational beliefs of faith removes a person from Islam, not mere sins or erroneous theological opinions.

Core Idea
Only denial of what brought you into faith takes you out of it.

The Ahl al-Sunna hold that no one from the qibla is excommunicated for major sins, yet they pronounce takfir on those who say the Qur'an is created or deny the beatific vision. How can both positions be consistent? This passage resolves the tension by citing the general rule: a servant leaves faith only by denying what brought him into it—namely, affirmation of tawhid and submission to everything known necessarily from the religion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the initial question about the Mu'tazilite and the apparent contradiction among Sunni scholars.
The passage uses technical terms like 'taghliz' (hyperbole) and 'juhud' (denial).
2
Identify the apparent contradiction
The author presents a problem: Sunni scholars say no one from the qibla is excommunicated, yet they excommunicate those who hold certain beliefs. Ask yourself how these statements can be reconciled.
This is a key issue in understanding takfir.
3
Understand the resolution and the rule
The author cites al-Bukhari's explanation that such pronouncements are hyperbole or conditional. Then the general rule is given: only juhud of what brought one into faith causes exit. Reflect on what this means for judging others.
The rule is quoted directly: 'لا يخرج العبد من الإيمان إلا بجحود ما أدخله فيه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يظهر الجواب عما قيل: إن المعتزلي إذا ارتكب كبيرة لزم أن يكون كافرا ليأسه من رحمة الله تعالى ولاعتقاده أنه ليس بمؤمن؛ وذلك لأنا لا نسلم أن اعتقاده استحقاقه النار يستلزم اليأس، وأن اعتقاده عدم إيمانه المفسر بمجموع التصديق والإقرار والأعمال بناء على انتفاء الأعمال يوجب الكفر. هذا والجمع بين قولهم: لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة، وقولهم: يكفر من قال بخلق القرآن واستحالة الرؤية أو سب الشيخين وأمثال ذلك مشكل. أقول: قد ذكر العلامة البخاري أن إطلاق مشايخنا الكفر بالكلمات المذكورة ونحوها ليس على ظاهره بل تغليظا يريدون به التنفير أو مقيد باعتقاد ما يكون به اللفظ كفرا. ويرشد إلى هذا قوله: ( ولا يخرج العبد من الإيمان إلا بجحود ما أدخله فيه ) أي في الإيمان وهو الإقرار بالتوحيد والإذعان به وبكل ما علم بالضرورة أنه من الدين.
And by this appears the answer to what was said: that the Mu'tazilite, if he commits a major sin, necessarily becomes a disbeliever due to his despair of Allah's mercy and his belief that he is not a believer; and that is because we do not concede that his belief that he deserves the Fire entails despair, and that his belief in the negation of his faith, defined as the sum of affirmation, confession, and deeds, based on the absence of deeds, necessitates disbelief. This, and reconciling between their saying: none of the people of the qibla is excommunicated, and their saying: whoever says that the Qur'an is created, or deems vision impossible, or insults the two shaykhs, and the like, is excommunicated, is problematic. I say: Al-'Allama al-Bukhari mentioned that our shaykhs' pronouncement of excommunication for the mentioned words and their like is not to be taken literally, but rather as a hyperbole intended to deter, or restricted to the belief that makes the word excommunication. And what points to this is his saying: 'And the servant does not leave faith except by denying what brought him into it' – that is, into faith, which is affirmation of tawhid and submission to it and to everything known by necessity to be from the religion.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is the author's own commentary resolving an apparent contradiction in the Ahl al-Sunna position. The issue is: how can they say no one from the qibla is excommunicated, yet excommunicate those who say the Qur'an is created, deny the beatific vision, or insult the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and 'Umar)? The author cites 'Allama al-Bukhari's explanation: such pronouncements of takfir are not literal but hyperbole (taghliz) to deter, or they are qualified by an underlying belief that makes the utterance actually disbelief. Then the author provides the general rule: a person does not leave faith except by juhud (denial) of what brought him into faith – namely, affirmation (iqrar) of tawhid and submission (idha'an) to it and to everything known by necessity to be from the religion. Thus, only outright denial of these essentials removes one from faith; sinful acts or utterances without denial do not.

Key Terms
تكفير
جحود
إيمان
توحيد
إذعان
تغليظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin or wrong theological opinion automatically makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only outright denial (juhud) of the foundational beliefs (tawhid and necessarily known matters) causes exit from faith.

Checkpoint
According to the rule cited in this passage, what is the only thing that removes a person from faith?
A) Committing a major sin
B) Holding a wrong theological opinion without denial
C) Denial (juhud) of what brought him into faith
D) Insulting the companions

Key Takeaway
A person remains within faith unless they deny the foundational beliefs of tawhid and what is necessarily known from the religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Response to the Objection Regarding Mu'tazilites and Major Sins
Maydani answers an objection about Mu'tazilites committing major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars respond to the objection that committing a major sin leads to disbelief, and the distinction between literal and hyperbolic declarations of disbelief.

Core Idea
Despair of mercy is not the same as believing one deserves punishment.

This lesson addresses a common objection against the Mu'tazilites about major sins. The objection claims that if a Mu'tazilite commits a major sin, he becomes a disbeliever due to despair and loss of faith. However, the response shows that despair is not entailed by belief in punishment, and that deeds are not part of the definition of faith. We also examine how scholars resolve apparent contradictions in declaring disbelief as hyperbole.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the objection, refutation, and resolution.
2
Understand the objection
Identify the two reasons given for why a Mu'tazilite committing a major sin would become a kafir: despair of mercy and belief of being a non-mu'min.
3
See the refutation
Notice how the author denies both premises: belief in deserving punishment does not entail despair, and lack of deeds does not entail kufr because deeds are not part of iman.
This is the core theological response.
4
Examine the inconsistency
Look at the apparent contradiction in Mu'tazilite statements about kufr and how al-Bukhari resolves it as hyperbole or conditioned on inner belief.
The Tahawiyyah principle supports this.
5
Reflect on the principle
Reflect on the Tahawiyyah principle: a slave only leaves faith by denying what brought him into it (confession of tawhid and submission).
This defines the boundary of kufr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يظهر الجواب عما قيل: إن المعتزلي إذا ارتكب كبيرة لزم أن يكون كافرا ليأسه من رحمة الله تعالى ولاعتقاده أنه ليس بمؤمن؛ وذلك لأنا لا نسلم أن اعتقاده استحقاقه النار يستلزم اليأس، وأن اعتقاده عدم إيمانه المفسر بمجموع التصديق والإقرار والأعمال بناء على انتفاء الأعمال يوجب الكفر. هذا والجمع بين قولهم: لا يكفر أحد من أهل القبلة، وقولهم: يكفر من قال بخلق القرآن واستحالة الرؤية أو سب الشيخين وأمثال ذلك مشكل اهـ. أقول: قد ذكر العلامة البخاري أن إطلاق مشايخنا الكفر بالكلمات المذكورة ونحوها ليس على ظاهره بل تغليظا يريدون به التنفير أو مقيد باعتقاد ما يكون به اللفظ كفرا. ويرشد إلى هذا قوله: ( ولا يخرج العبد من الإيمان إلا بجحود ما أي الذي أدخله فيه ) أي في الإيمان وهو الإقرار بالتوحيد والإذعان به وبكل ما علم بالضرورة أنه من الدين.
And with this appears the answer to what was said: If the Mu'tazilite commits a major sin, he necessarily becomes a disbeliever, due to his despair of the mercy of God Almighty and his belief that he is not a believer; and that is because we do not concede that his belief that he deserves the fire necessitates despair, and that his belief that he is not a believer—interpreted as the sum total of attestation, confession, and deeds—on the basis of the absence of deeds necessitates disbelief. This, and reconciling their statement: 'None of the people of the qibla is a disbeliever' with their statement: 'One who says the Qur'an is created, or that vision of God is impossible, or reviles the two shaykhs and the like is a disbeliever' is problematic. I say: al-Allama al-Bukhari mentioned that the application of 'disbelief' by our shaykhs to the mentioned words and the like is not literal, but rather a hyperbole by which they intend deterrence or it is conditional upon a belief that makes the word disbelief. And this is guided by his saying: 'The slave does not leave faith except by denial of that which brought him into it'—that is, into faith—which is confession of tawhid and submission to it and to everything known necessarily to be from religion.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that if a Mu'tazilite commits a major sin (kabira), he necessarily becomes a kafir for two reasons: he despairs of God's mercy (because he believes he will be eternally punished) and he believes he is not a believer (since he lacks deeds). The answer denies both premises: first, believing one deserves punishment does not entail despair (yas)—one may still hope for forgiveness; second, considering oneself a non-believer due to lack of deeds does not entail kufr because deeds are not part of the definition of iman. The author then notes an apparent inconsistency among the Mu'tazilites: they say no one from the qibla is kafir, yet they declare kufr for those who say the Quran is created, deny the beatific vision, or insult the first two caliphs. To resolve this, the author cites al-Bukhari's explanation that such declarations of kufr by scholars are not meant literally but as hyperbole or conditioned on an inner belief that would make the statement truly kufr. This is supported by the Tahawiyyah text: a slave only leaves faith by denying what brought him into it, i.e., confession of tawhid and submission to all necessarily known matters of religion.

كبيرة
المعتزلي
الكفر
اليأس
التغليظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that one deserves punishment for sins is the same as despairing of God's mercy.
Correction: The passage explicitly denies this equivalence: one may believe one deserves punishment yet still hope for forgiveness through God's grace.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Mu'tazilite's belief that he deserves punishment not necessarily make him a kafir?
A) Because he may still hope for forgiveness.
B) Because he does not actually believe he deserves punishment.
C) Because he is from ahl al-qibla.
D) Because he has not committed a major sin.

Key Takeaway
Declarations of disbelief by scholars are often hyperbole, not literal, and the true criterion for leaving faith is denial of fundamental beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Reflect on your own understanding of faith. Do you ever doubt your own iman because of your shortcomings in deeds? How does the Tahawiyyah principle—that one only leaves faith by denying fundamental beliefs—change your self-evaluation?

Definition of Iman and the Schools' Positions
Maydani defines iman and presents different views on its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of iman according to the Sunni perspective, its two essential components (iqrar and tasdiq), and how different theological schools define iman differently.

Core Idea
Iman is both confession by the tongue and attestation by the heart; neither alone suffices.

This lesson begins with the matn's definition of iman and then delves into the commentary's elaboration. Maydani explains that true faith involves both outward confession and inner conviction. He also surveys the views of various schools—Ash'aris, Khawarij, Karramiyya, and Hanafis—to clarify the precise nature of iman and its pillars.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of iman
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the core definition: iman is confession by the tongue and attestation by the heart.
Notice that the matn immediately links iman to acceptance of all revelation.
2
Understand the two pillars
The commentary explains that both iqrar and tasdiq are pillars, but tasdiq is essential and cannot be dropped, while iqrar can be dropped under coercion.
This distinction is crucial for understanding cases like forced renunciation.
3
Distinguish general vs. detailed belief
Learn the difference between matters known generally (like belief in angels) which require only general assent, and specific details (like naming Jibril) which require specific belief.
Failure to believe in a specific prophet or angel constitutes disbelief.
4
Survey the schools' definitions
Read the discussion of the Ash'ari, Khawarij, Karramiyya, and Hanafi positions on the components of iman.
Compare how each school emphasizes different components and why.
5
Reflect on the Ash'ari verifiers' synthesis
Consider the argument that since tasdiq can be by tongue as well as heart, both are pillars except in cases of inability, reconciling the views.
This shows how later scholars harmonized different positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالإِيمَانُ هُوَ الإِقْرَارُ بِاللُّسَانِ وَالتَّصْدِيقُ بِالجَنَانِ وَإِنَّ جَمِيعَ مَا أَنْزَلَ اللَّهُ فِي القُرْآنِ وَجَمِيعَ مَا صَحَّ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم مِنَ الشَّرْعِ وَالبَيَانِ كُلُّهُ حَقٌّ. والإيمان هو أي حقيقته الإقرار بالوحدانية وحقية الرسالة باللسان والتصديق بالجنان أي قبول القلب وإذعانه لما علم بالضرورة أنه من دين النبي بحيث تعلمه العامة من غير افتقار إلى نظر واستدلال؛ كالوحدانية والنبوة والبعث والجزاء ووجوب الصلاة والزكاة وحرمة الخمر ونحوها، ويكفي الإجمال فيما يلاحظ إجمالاً؛ كالإيمان بالملائكة والكتب والرسل، ويشترط التفصيل فيما يلاحظ تفصيلا كجبريل وميكائيل وموسى وعيسى والتوراة والإنجيل، حتى إن من لم يصدق بواحد معين منها كافر. واعلم أن كلا منهما ركن إلا أن التصديق ركن لا يحتمل السقوط أصلا والإقرار قد يحتمله كما في حالة الإكراه والعجز، وفي المسايرة في أول الخاتمة في بحث الإيمان ومفهومه فقيل: هو التصديق بالقلب فقط، وهو المختار عند جمهور الأشاعرة، أو مع الطاعة، وهو قول الخوارج؛ ولذا كفّروا بالذنب لانتقاء جزء الماهية، أو باللسان فقط وهو قول الكرامية؛ فإن طابق تصديق القلب فهو مؤمن ناج وإلا فهو مؤمن مخلد في النار، أو بالقلب واللسان وهو منقول عن أبي حنيفة ومشهور عن أصحابه، وبعض المحققين من الأشاعرة قالوا: لما كان الإيمان التصديق، والتصديق كما يكون بالقلب يكون باللسان فيكون كل منهما ركنا في الباب فلا يثبت الإيمان إلا بهما إلا عند العجز، وكذا الاحتياط واقع عليه والنصوص دالة عليه، وذكروا ما تعلقت به؛ يعني الكرامية؛ يعني من نحو قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام: «أُمِرْتُ أَنْ أُقَاتِلَ النَّاسَ حَتَّى يَقُولُوا لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا
And faith (al-iman) is confession by the tongue and attestation (al-tasdiq) by the heart (al-janan), and indeed all that God has revealed in the Qur'an and all that has been authentically transmitted from the Prophet ﷺ of law and explanation—all of it is truth. And al-iman is—i.e., its reality—confession of the oneness of God and the truth of the message by the tongue and attestation by the heart, i.e., the acceptance and submission of the heart to what is known necessarily to be from the religion of the Prophet, such that the common people know it without needing reflection and deduction: such as oneness, prophethood, resurrection, recompense, the obligation of prayer and zakat, the prohibition of wine, and the like. And general (al-ijmal) suffices for what is observed in general, like faith in the angels, books, and messengers; but detail (al-tafsil) is required for what is observed in detail, such as Jibril, Mika'il, Musa, 'Isa, the Torah, and the Gospel, such that one who does not believe in any specific one of them is a disbeliever. And know that both are pillars, except that attestation is a pillar that cannot possibly fall at all, while confession may possibly fall as in the case of coercion and inability. And in al-Musayara at the beginning of the conclusion in the discussion of faith and its concept, it is said: it is attestation by the heart only—and this is the chosen view according to the majority of Ash'aris—or with obedience, which is the view of the Khawarij; thus they declare disbelief upon sin due to the missing of a part of the essence; or by the tongue only, which is the view of the Karramiyya; if it corresponds to the attestation of the heart, he is a successful believer, otherwise he is a believer eternally in the Fire ; or by heart and tongue, which is transmitted from Abu Hanifa and famous from his followers. And some of the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) from the Ash'aris said: Since faith is attestation, and attestation is just as it is by the heart so it is by the tongue, then each of them is a pillar in this topic, so faith is not established except by both except in the case of inability. And likewise caution is based upon it, and the texts indicate it, and they mentioned what the Karramiyya relied upon, i.e., from such as his saying ﷺ: 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa...'
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage first states the definition of iman from the matn: 'Iman is confession by the tongue and attestation by the heart, and all revelation is true.' Then the commentary elaborates: 'Iman is (i.e., its essence) confession of monotheism and the truth of the message by the tongue and attestation by the heart—meaning the heart's acceptance and submission to what is necessarily known from the Prophet's religion, such that even common people know it without reasoning. It then distinguishes between matters known in general (like belief in angels, books, messengers) which require only general assent, and matters known in detail (like specific angels and prophets) which require specific belief; failure to believe in any specific one is kufr. The author then notes that both iqrar and tasdiq are pillars (rukn), but tasdiq is essential and can never be dropped, while iqrar may be dropped in cases of coercion or incapacity. The passage then surveys different schools on the definition of iman: the Ash'aris (tasdiq only), Khawarij (tasdiq plus deeds, leading to takfir upon major sin), Karramiyya (iqrar only, with the odd consequence that a hypocrite is a believer but doomed to hell), Abu Hanifa (both heart and tongue). Finally, some Ash'ari verifiers argue that since tasdiq can be by tongue as well as heart, both are pillars for the establishment of iman, except in cases of inability; this is supported by caution and texts, including the hadith 'I was commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa...' which the Karramiyya cite.

Key Terms
الإيمان
الإقرار
التصديق
الجنان
الركن
الإجمال
التفصيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that iman is only by the tongue (iqrar) without requiring inner conviction (tasdiq).
Correction: The passage states that both iqrar and tasdiq are pillars; tasdiq is essential and can never fall, while iqrar alone (as the Karramiyya claim) leads to the problematic conclusion that a hypocrite is a believer but eternally punished.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which component of iman can never fall even in cases of coercion?
A) Iqrar bil-lisan (confession by the tongue)
B) Tasdiq bil-janan (attestation by the heart)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Iman requires both inner conviction (tasdiq bil-janan) and outward expression (iqrar bil-lisan); while iqrar can be excused under duress, inner belief is indispensable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever professed faith with your tongue while your heart held back from full submission? How does knowing that tasdiq is a pillar that can never fall challenge you to examine your inner certainty?
?
The Karramiyya argued that even a hypocrite is a believer. Do you ever act like the hypocrites—outwardly compliant but inwardly resistant? What does your private life reveal about your true iman?
?
The Khawarij declared sinners disbelievers. Do you judge fellow Muslims who commit major sins, forgetting that your own faith depends on Allah's mercy? How can you cultivate humility and hope, avoiding both extremes?

Definition of Iman: Tongue and Heart
The commentator defines iman and explains its components and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman consists of verbal affirmation and heartfelt belief, and that belief in specific matters requires specific knowledge.

Core Idea
Iman is spoken with the tongue and believed in the heart.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the essential components of iman (faith) according to Ahl al-Sunnah. He explains that true iman is not merely a verbal declaration but also a heartfelt conviction. Moreover, he distinguishes between matters that require general belief and those that demand detailed knowledge, warning that denial of any specific prophet or scripture is tantamount to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the key terms: iqrar, tasdiq, janan.
2
Identify the two components of iman
Find where the commentator states that iman is 'affirmation with the tongue' and 'belief with the heart'. Understand that both are necessary.
The heart's belief is described as acceptance and submission.
3
Understand general vs. detailed belief
Notice the distinction: general belief suffices for broad categories (angels, books, messengers), but detailed belief is required for specific individuals (Jibril, Musa, etc.) and scriptures.
The passage gives examples of each.
4
Recognize the consequence of denial
The passage explicitly states that not believing in a specific messenger or scripture is disbelief. This underscores the importance of detailed belief.
5
Reflect on application
Consider how this distinction affects a person's faith. Why is it not enough to say 'I believe in all messengers' without knowing who they are?
This leads to the core idea that iman requires knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالإِيمَانُ هُوَ الإِقْرَارُ بِاللُّسَانِ وَالتَّصْدِيقُ بِالجَنَانِ وَإِنَّ جَمِيعَ مَا أَنْزَلَ اللَّهُ فِي القُرْآنِ وَجَمِيعَ مَا صَحَّ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم مِنَ الشَّرْعِ وَالبَيَانِ كُلُّهُ حَقٌّ. وَالإيمان هو (أي حقيقته) الإقرار بالوحدانية وحقية الرسالة باللسان والتصديق بالجنان أي قبول القلب وإذعانه لما علم بالضرورة أنه من دين النبي بحيث تعلمه العامة من غير افتقار إلى نظر واستدلال؛ كالوحدانية والنبوة والبعث والجزاء ووجوب الصلاة والزكاة وحرمة الخمر ونحوها، ويكفي الإجمال فيما يلاحظ إجمالاً؛ كالإيمان بالملائكة والكتب والرسل، ويشترط التفصيل فيما يلاحظ تفصيلا كجبريل وميكائيل وموسى وعيسى والتوراة والإنجيل، حتى إن من لم يصدق بواحد معين منها كافر.
And iman is affirmation with the tongue and belief with the heart. And indeed all that Allah sent down in the Qur'an and all that is authentically reported from the Prophet, peace be upon him, of the law and explanation, all of it is true. And iman is (i.e., its reality) affirmation of the oneness of Allah and the truth of the message with the tongue, and belief with the heart, meaning acceptance of the heart and its submission to what is known by necessity to be from the religion of the Prophet, such that the general public learn it without needing reflection and inference; like oneness, prophethood, resurrection, recompense, the obligation of prayer, zakat, the prohibition of wine, and the like. And general belief suffices in matters that are taken generally, like belief in angels, books, and messengers, while detailed belief is required in matters that are taken in detail, like Jibril, Mika'il, Musa, 'Isa, the Torah, and the Gospel, to the extent that whoever does not believe in a specific one of them is a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage defines iman and explains its components. The commentator states that iman consists of two elements: iqrar (verbal affirmation) with the tongue and tasdiq (belief) with the heart. All revelation and authentic prophetic teachings are true and must be accepted. The reality of iman is then clarified: it is affirming tawhid and the truth of the message verbally, while the heart accepts and submits to what is known by necessity from the Prophet's religion. Such matters are known to the general public without need for deep reflection, e.g., tawhid, prophethood, resurrection, prayer, zakat, prohibition of wine, etc. The commentator then distinguishes between general and detailed belief: for matters that are general concepts (angels, books, messengers), general belief suffices; for specific individuals (Jibril, Mika'il, Musa, 'Isa) and specific scriptures (Torah, Gospel), detailed belief is required. Failure to believe in a specific one cited constitutes disbelief.

Key Terms
إيمان
إقرار
تصديق
جنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a general, vague belief in 'the messengers' or 'the angels' is sufficient, without needing to know or believe in each specifically.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that detailed belief is required for specific individuals like Jibril, Musa, etc., and that failure to believe in a specific one renders one a disbeliever.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who does not believe in a specific messenger like Musa?
A) He is still a believer
B) He is a disbeliever
C) It depends on his intention
D) Only if he rejects intentionally

Key Takeaway
Iman combines verbal affirmation and heartfelt belief; while general belief suffices for broad categories, specific belief in each named prophet, angel, and scripture is required, and denying any one is disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered whether your heart truly submits to all the specific details of faith, such as the names and roles of each angel and prophet? If not, what will you do today to learn and affirm them sincerely?
?
When you recite the Shahada with your tongue, do you ever pause to ask your heart if it fully accepts everything that comes with it, including the obligation to know and believe in every specific messenger?
?
Is there any specific article of faith mentioned in this lesson that you have not yet fully internalized? Make a firm intention to study it until your heart is certain, for denying even one is disbelief.
